After soaping it up and cleansing my face, I rinse it out really well: I lay one hand out flat with the palm up and the sponge on my hand and hold it under running water while gently pressing downwards on top of it with my other hand, not all the way down but enough to push out some water and suds and suck in the fresh water. I keep doing this until no more suds are coming out. Then I gently press together 1-3 times outside of water to get most of the water out. DO NOT WRING IT or squeeze it tightly. I then press it lightly in between a towel 2 times using the same hand method as above and then hang it on its string on a hook to air dry. ALWAYS AIR DRY this.   449. Typically you use charcoal, also called carbon, to remove organic chemicals from substances like water. So, as you might easily imagine, using this sponge with chlorinated water will quickly render the minuscule amount of charcoal in these sponges useless as the charcoal is rapidly used up as the chlorine is absorbed. The manufacturer apparently knows this and neglects to put any information about the useful life of the charcoal on their packaging. A glaring omission is stating how long the charcoal lasts. Obviously, there are no benefits, so they avoid mentioning any. As far as the texture of the sponge goes, I think it's great. It's soft and lasts for 2 weeks maximum with 2 face washings per day. After 2 weeks the surface breaks down and at that point it becomes ineffective. It's clear that the manufacturer is using charcoal as a gimmick. The charcoal incorrectly adds perceived value to what should be a very, very inexpensive product. For me the value of these sponges at first was pretty high, but after analyzing the facts I feel that these sponges would be properly valued at 1/4 their cost.
